  v0.10.5 — Aura_Benchmark
  DeepSeek · deepseek/deepseek-chat · Unknown · auto mode · chat fb40a8e9-mrva4tk6
  /run/media/dusan/DATA1/Aura_Benchmark
  "I don't try. I verify."
────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Task: A model produces a confident, detailed, well-formatted answer to a question about a file that doesn't exist. The answer passes a basic 'is it non-empty?' check. Why is this a dangerous failure mode, and what specifically was built to catch it?

  ◆ thinking…  ◆ thinking…  ◆ Context: █░░░░░░░░░░┊░░┊░░┊░░ 3% (4.1k/128k) · compact @ 55% (70k) · 1 turns

This is a **fabrication** (hallucination) failure mode, and it's dangerous because:

1. **It bypasses naive quality checks** — a non-empty, well-formatted answer looks valid to any simple "does it have content?" filter. The model sounds authoritative while being completely wrong.
2. **It wastes trust** — if undetected, these fabricated answers pollute the benchmark results, making the model look competent when it actually failed.
3. **It's hard to catch automatically** — you can't grep for "does this file exist" because the model invents plausible filenames and paths that sound real.

The specific mechanism built to catch this is the **verification step** in the Ruby Alternator architecture. The large model (GLM-5.2) acts as a verifier that checks Ruby's answers against reality. The case study at `case-studies/2026-07-17-fabrication-catch.md` documents exactly this scenario.

Let me read it to confirm the details:


  📄 read_file  case-studies/2026-07-17-fabrication-catch.md
  ✓ case-studies/2026-07-17-fabrication-catch.md (114 lines): (+115 lines) 1ms
  ◆ thinking…  ◆ Context: █░░░░░░░░░░┊░░┊░░┊░░ 4% (5.6k/128k) · compact @ 55% (70k) · 2 turns

Here's the full picture:

## Why it's dangerous

A fabricated answer that's **confident, detailed, well-formatted, and non-empty** passes every surface-level quality check. It looks like a correct answer. The danger is that:

- **It's invisible to automated scoring** — the benchmark's basic "is it non-empty?" filter sees content and marks it as a pass.
- **It inflates pass rates** — the model looks competent when it actually hallucinated.
- **It erodes trust in the benchmark** — if fabrications go undetected, the entire evaluation becomes meaningless.
- **It's a silent failure** — unlike a crash or empty response, no alarm triggers.

## What was built to catch it

The **verification gate** in the Ruby Alternator architecture was extended with a specific anti-fabrication check. The key change was:

1. **Before the fix:** The verifier (large cloud model) only checked "does this answer address the task completely?" — judging **shape**, not **truth**.

2. **After the fix:** The verifier receives **Ruby's own tool-call history** — what it searched for and what came back — and is explicitly instructed to check for **direct contradictions**:

   > *"If a tool result says a function/file/symbol was not found, but the answer describes it in detail as if it exists, that is a fabrication and must be marked INVALID regardless of how complete or well-written the answer looks."*

This is documented in detail at **`case-studies/2026-07-17-fabrication-catch.md`** — and proven live: the same trap was re-run with a different nonexistent function, Ruby fabricated again, and this time the verifier caught it and escalated to the cloud model, which honestly reported the function doesn't exist.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  ✓ Done
  2 turns · 1 tool call

  Here's the full picture:
  
  ## Why it's dangerous
  
  A fabricated answer that's **confident, detailed, well-formatted, and non-empty** passes every surface-level quality check. It looks like a correct answer. The danger is that:
  
  - **It's invisible to automated scoring** — the benchmark's basic "is it non-empty?" filter sees content and marks it as a pass.
  - **It inflates pass rates** — the model looks competent when it actually hallucinated.
  - **It erodes trust in the benchmark** — if fabrications go undetected, the entire evaluation becomes meaningless.
  - **It's a silent failure** — unlike a crash or empty response, no alarm triggers.
  
  ## What was built to catch it
  
  The **verification gate** in the Ruby Alternator architecture was extended with a specific anti-fabrication check. The key change was:
  
  1. **Before the fix:** The verifier (large cloud model) only checked "does this answer address the task completely?" — judging **shape**, not **truth**.
  
  2. **After the fix:** The verifier receives **Ruby's own tool-call history** — what it searched for and what came back — and is explicitly instructed to check for **direct contradictions**:
  
     > *"If a tool result says a function/file/symbol was not found, but the answer describes it in detail as if it exists, that is a fabrication and must be marked INVALID regardless of how complete or well-written the answer looks."*
  
  This is documented in detail at **`case-studies/2026-07-17-fabrication-catch.md`** — and proven live: the same trap was re-run with a different nonexistent function, Ruby fabricated again, and this time the verifier caught it and escalated to the cloud model, which honestly reported the function doesn't exist.

Now I have the full picture. Here's the answer:

---

## Why Ruby must never inherit `--auto` permission

A local small model like Ruby (Granite 4.1 3B) must **always** run read-only, regardless of the session's `--auto` flag, for one concrete reason: **it is unproven and prone to confident fabrication**, and a real incident proved that write access was dangerous.

### The incident (2026-07-17)

Ruby was asked about a function called `readAndValidate` in `src/agent/loop.ts`. Ruby's own `search_code` tool returned **"No results"** — yet Ruby fabricated a 600-word detailed answer with fake TypeScript implementation, fake parameters, and a fake step-by-step table. Because Ruby was running with the session's write permissions (including `--auto`, which skips confirmation), an earlier version of this bug included Ruby **actually writing a hallucinated comment into a real source file**.

### The principle

The design rule (from tier 5 question t5-05 and tier 7 question t7-08) is:

> **A small, unproven model should never inherit the session's permission level** — even when the user explicitly launched with `--auto`. Competence must be established first. Until the model proves it can handle a task pattern reliably (tracked via episode history and competence scoring), it stays read-only. This prevents a single hallucination from causing real damage to the codebase.

### The enforcement

Ruby's read-only sandbox is enforced at the **tool dispatcher level as a hard gate** — not a system-prompt request. This means even if prompt injection or a hallucination tells Ruby to write a file, the `PermissionDeniedError` from the tool execution layer blocks it. The session's `--auto` flag controls the *user's* permission level for the large cloud model; Ruby's permission level is independently locked to read-only regardless.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  ✓ Done
  5 turns · 7 tool calls

  Now I have the full picture. Here's the answer:
  
  ---
  
  ## Why Ruby must never inherit `--auto` permission
  
  A local small model like Ruby (Granite 4.1 3B) must **always** run read-only, regardless of the session's `--auto` flag, for one concrete reason: **it is unproven and prone to confident fabrication**, and a real incident proved that write access was dangerous.
  
  ### The incident (2026-07-17)
  
  Ruby was asked about a function called `readAndValidate` in `src/agent/loop.ts`. Ruby's own `search_code` tool returned **"No results"** — yet Ruby fabricated a 600-word detailed answer with fake TypeScript implementation, fake parameters, and a fake step-by-step table. Because Ruby was running with the session's write permissions (including `--auto`, which skips confirmation), an earlier version of this bug included Ruby **actually writing a hallucinated comment into a real source file**.
  
  ### The principle
  
  The design rule (from tier 5 question t5-05 and tier 7 question t7-08) is:
  
  > **A small, unproven model should never inherit the session's permission level** — even when the user explicitly launched with `--auto`. Competence must be established first. Until the model proves it can handle a task pattern reliably (tracked via episode history and competence scoring), it stays read-only. This prevents a single hallucination from causing real damage to the codebase.
  
  ### The enforcement
  
  Ruby's read-only sandbox is enforced at the **tool dispatcher level as a hard gate** — not a system-prompt request. This means even if prompt injection or a hallucination tells Ruby to write a file, the `PermissionDeniedError` from the tool execution layer blocks it. The session's `--auto` flag controls the *user's* permission level for the large cloud model; Ruby's permission level is independently locked to read-only regardless.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Task: A Ruby Alternator session has these stats: total=50, rubySuccesses=8, rubyFailures=32, largeModelInterventions=42. Calculate Ruby's actual success rate among attempts, explain whether fine-tuning is warranted, and suggest what the most likely root cause is given the ratio.

  ◆ thinking…  ◆ thinking…  ◆ Context: █░░░░░░░░░░┊░░┊░░┊░░ 3% (3.9k/128k) · compact @ 55% (70k) · 1 turns

Let me analyze the stats carefully.

**Given:**
- total = 50 (total questions in the session)
- rubySuccesses = 8
- rubyFailures = 32
- largeModelInterventions = 42

**Step 1 — Understand the relationship between these numbers**

The total questions = 50. Ruby attempted some, and the large model handled the rest.

- rubySuccesses + rubyFailures = 8 + 32 = 40. This means Ruby attempted 40 out of 50 questions.
- largeModelInterventions = 42. But if Ruby only failed 32, why 42 interventions?

The discrepancy tells us something: **largeModelInterventions counts every time the large model was called**, which includes:
1. All 32 Ruby failures (escalated to large model)
2. All 8 Ruby successes (verified by large model — the verification step)
3. Possibly 2 more calls (e.g., retries, re-verifications, or questions Ruby never attempted at all)

So: 32 (failures escalated) + 8 (successes verified) = 40. The extra 2 interventions could be re-verifications or questions where Ruby produced no output at all.

**Step 2 — Ruby's actual success rate among attempts**

Ruby attempted 40 questions. It succeeded on 8.

**Ruby success rate = 8 / 40 = 20%**

**Step 3 — Is fine-tuning warranted?**

Yes, absolutely. A 20% success rate means Ruby fails 4 out of every 5 attempts. This is far below any reasonable threshold for a useful assistant. Fine-tuning is warranted if:

- The failure pattern is **systematic** (same category of questions always fails) — fine-tuning on those categories could help.
- The failure pattern is **random** (fails across all categories) — fine-tuning is less likely to help; the base model may simply be too small (3.4B parameters) for the task complexity.

**Step 4 — Most likely root cause given the ratio**

The key ratio is **RubySuccesses : RubyFailures = 8 : 32 = 1 : 4**.

This is a **80% failure rate** — not a subtle degradation, but near-total incompetence. The most likely root causes, in order:

1. **Model capacity ceiling (most likely):** Granite 4.1 3B is a 3.4B-parameter model. The benchmark includes complex tasks (implementation, multi-step reasoning, domain-specific knowledge). A 3B model simply lacks the capacity to handle these reliably. The 20% success rate is consistent with a model that can answer trivial/general-knowledge questions but fails on anything requiring multi-step reasoning or project-specific context.

2. **Context/knowledge gap:** The lessons file explicitly states Ruby "fails on every Aura-specific knowledge question" and "fails on nearly all implementation tasks." If the benchmark is heavy on these categories, the 80% failure rate is explained by Ruby having zero access to project docs or runtime context.

3. **Not a fine-tuning problem:** The failures are not subtle — they're catastrophic. Fine-tuning a 3B model to fix a 20% success rate would require massive, high-quality datasets across all failure categories. It's more likely the model is being asked to do things it fundamentally cannot do at its size.

**Conclusion:** Fine-tuning is warranted only if the failure categories are narrow and well-defined. But given the 80% failure rate and the documented pattern (Ruby fails on *all* Aura-specific and implementation tasks), the root cause is **model capacity** — the 3B model is too small for the benchmark's complexity. The fix is either (a) a larger local model, or (b) a smarter escalation strategy that routes known-hard categories directly to the large model without wasting Ruby's attempts.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  1. All 32 Ruby failures (escalated to large model)
  2. All 8 Ruby successes (verified by large model — the verification step)
  3. Possibly 2 more calls (e.g., retries, re-verifications, or questions Ruby never attempted at all)

  **Step 2 — Ruby's actual success rate among attempts**
  
  Ruby attempted 40 questions. It succeeded on 8.

  - The failure pattern is **systematic** (same category of questions always fails) — fine-tuning on those categories could help.

  This is a **80% failure rate** — not a subtle degradation, but near-total incompetence. The most likely root causes, in order:
  
  1. **Model capacity ceiling (most likely):** Granite 4.1 3B is a 3.4B-parameter model. The benchmark includes complex tasks (implementation, multi-step reasoning, domain-specific knowledge). If the benchmark is heavy on these categories, the 80% failure rate is explained by Ruby having zero access to project docs or runtime context.
  
  3. **Not a fine-tuning problem:** The failures are not subtle — they're catastrophic. ---

## Three Most Important Metrics to Prove an AI Coding Agent Improves Over Time

These are drawn from the actual architecture of this project (Aura 50-Day Trial), which is a real, running experiment designed to answer exactly this question.

---

### 1. Benchmark Pass Rate (by tier)

**What it proves:** The agent's ability to produce correct answers on a fixed, repeatable question set over time. An upward trend in pass rate (especially on harder tiers) is the most direct evidence that the agent's knowledge, reasoning, or tool-use is improving. In this project, tiers 1–4 test factual recall, tiers 5–19 test implementation, research, and review tasks — so tier-specific pass rates isolate which capabilities are improving.

**What it cannot prove:** That the improvement generalizes beyond the benchmark. A fixed question set can be memorized or pattern-matched without genuine capability growth. It also cannot prove the agent is improving on *real* tasks — only on the curated ones in the suite.

**How it can be gamed:** Train or prompt-engineer specifically to the benchmark questions. Leak the question bank into the agent's training data or system prompt. Overfit hyperparameters to maximize benchmark score while degrading on unseen tasks. In this project, the benchmark is run daily with the same questions — if the agent's memory layer accumulates the *answers* rather than the *competence*, the pass rate will rise from memorization, not understanding.

---

### 2. Verification-Gate Catch Rate

**What it proves:** How often the system's verification layer (a stronger model reviewing the small model's output) correctly identifies and escalates a bad answer. This is the most honest metric in the Aura architecture: a *dropping* catch rate over time is evidence the small model is making fewer mistakes that need catching. It directly measures the gap between the small model and the large model — when that gap shrinks, the catch rate falls.

**What it cannot prove:** That the small model is getting *better* rather than the large model getting *worse* or more lenient. A dropping catch rate could also mean the verifier is fatigued, has context-window issues, or is being less rigorous. It also cannot distinguish between "the small model answered correctly" and "the small model made a mistake the verifier missed."

**How it can be gamed:** Weaken the verifier — use a smaller or less capable model, reduce its context window, or prompt it to be more permissive ("only escalate if the answer is completely wrong"). In this project, the verifier is a fixed model (DeepSeek Chat / GLM-5.2), but if the verifier's prompt is silently changed to require higher confidence before escalating, the catch rate drops artificially, making the small model look better than it is.

---

### 3. Competence-by-Category Trend

**What it proves:** Whether improvement is broad or narrow. Breaking down success rates by task category (implementation, research, review, refactor) reveals if the agent is genuinely getting better at coding or just getting better at one type of question. A flat implementation score but rising research score tells a very different story than uniform improvement across all categories.

**What it cannot prove:** That the categories are correctly labeled or that the difficulty within a category is consistent across tiers. A category like "implementation" might contain both trivial one-liners and complex multi-file refactors — averaging them hides the real picture. It also cannot prove that the agent is learning transferable skills rather than memorizing category-specific patterns.

**How it can be gamed:** Redefine category boundaries after the fact to make trends look better. Move hard questions out of a struggling category into a different one. Add easy questions to a category to inflate its score. In this project, the categories are defined by the question's `tier` field — if someone reclassifies a tier-15 (hard implementation) question as tier-5 (easy recall), the "implementation" category appears to improve without any actual capability change.

---

### Why These Three Together

No single metric is trustworthy. The benchmark pass rate shows *what* the agent produces, the verification catch rate shows *how often it needs help*, and the category breakdown shows *where* improvement is (or isn't) happening. Cross-referencing them catches gaming attempts: a rising pass rate with a steady catch rate suggests memorization (the verifier is still catching the same fraction of mistakes); a rising pass rate with a falling catch rate suggests genuine improvement (fewer mistakes to catch). The category breakdown prevents the team from cherry-picking which tasks to optimize.

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Now I have a thorough understanding of the project's actual architecture. Here's my answer:
  
  ---
  
  ## Three Most Important Metrics to Prove an AI Coding Agent Improves Over Time
  
  These are drawn from the actual architecture of this project (Aura 50-Day Trial), which is a real, running experiment designed to answer exactly this question.
  
  ---
  
  ### 1. Benchmark Pass Rate (by tier)
  
  **What it proves:** The agent's ability to produce correct answers on a fixed, repeatable question set over time. An upward trend in pass rate (especially on harder tiers) is the most direct evidence that the agent's knowledge, reasoning, or tool-use is improving. In this project, tiers 1–4 test factual recall, tiers 5–19 test implementation, research, and review tasks — so tier-specific pass rates isolate which capabilities are improving.
  
  **What it cannot prove:** That the improvement generalizes beyond the benchmark. A fixed question set can be memorized or pattern-matched without genuine capability growth. It also cannot prove the agent is improving on *real* tasks — only on the curated ones in the suite.
  
  **How it can be gamed:** Train or prompt-engineer specifically to the benchmark questions. Leak the question bank into the agent's training data or system prompt. Overfit hyperparameters to maximize benchmark score while degrading on unseen tasks. In this project, the benchmark is run daily with the same questions — if the agent's memory layer accumulates the *answers* rather than the *competence*, the pass rate will rise from memorization, not understanding.
  
  ---
  
  ### 2. Verification-Gate Catch Rate
  
  **What it proves:** How often the system's verification layer (a stronger model reviewing the small model's output) correctly identifies and escalates a bad answer. This is the most honest metric in the Aura architecture: a *dropping* catch rate over time is evidence the small model is making fewer mistakes that need catching. It directly measures the gap between the small model and the large model — when that gap shrinks, the catch rate falls.
  
  **What it cannot prove:** That the small model is getting *better* rather than the large model getting *worse* or more lenient. A dropping catch rate could also mean the verifier is fatigued, has context-window issues, or is being less rigorous. It also cannot distinguish between "the small model answered correctly" and "the small model made a mistake the verifier missed."
  
  **How it can be gamed:** Weaken the verifier — use a smaller or less capable model, reduce its context window, or prompt it to be more permissive ("only escalate if the answer is completely wrong"). In this project, the verifier is a fixed model (DeepSeek Chat / GLM-5.2), but if the verifier's prompt is silently changed to require higher confidence before escalating, the catch rate drops artificially, making the small model look better than it is.
  
  ---
  
  ### 3. Competence-by-Category Trend
  
  **What it proves:** Whether improvement is broad or narrow. Breaking down success rates by task category (implementation, research, review, refactor) reveals if the agent is genuinely getting better at coding or just getting better at one type of question. A flat implementation score but rising research score tells a very different story than uniform improvement across all categories.
  
  **What it cannot prove:** That the categories are correctly labeled or that the difficulty within a category is consistent across tiers. A category like "implementation" might contain both trivial one-liners and complex multi-file refactors — averaging them hides the real picture. It also cannot prove that the agent is learning transferable skills rather than memorizing category-specific patterns.
  
  **How it can be gamed:** Redefine category boundaries after the fact to make trends look better. Move hard questions out of a struggling category into a different one. Add easy questions to a category to inflate its score. In this project, the categories are defined by the question's `tier` field — if someone reclassifies a tier-15 (hard implementation) question as tier-5 (easy recall), the "implementation" category appears to improve without any actual capability change.
  
  ---
  
  ### Why These Three Together
  
  No single metric is trustworthy. The benchmark pass rate shows *what* the agent produces, the verification catch rate shows *how often it needs help*, and the category breakdown shows *where* improvement is (or isn't) happening. Cross-referencing them catches gaming attempts: a rising pass rate with a steady catch rate suggests memorization (the verifier is still catching the same fraction of mistakes); a rising pass rate with a falling catch rate suggests genuine improvement (fewer mistakes to catch). The category breakdown prevents the team from cherry-picking which tasks to optimize.
